                                 1 0
    to this off-diagonal perturbation (by symmetry; it appears at second order). (c) When two eigenvalues
    nearly coincide (λk ≈ λj ), the eigenvectors ⃗xk and ⃗xj become nearly parallel, making ⃗ykT ⃗xk ≈ 0 in the
    denominator of (C.2). The sensitivity diverges, signaling that eigenvalue perturbation theory breaks down,
    the same mathematical mechanism that causes SA indices to diverge near a bifurcation (Chapter 8).
130APPENDIX C. EIGENVALUE SENSITIVITY AND SINGULAR VALUE DECOMPOSITION
Appendix D

Periodic Solutions and Floquet Theory

For models with periodic forcing or limit-cycle behavior, SA of the periodic orbit requires tools
beyond those developed in Chapter 4. This appendix introduces Floquet theory and the sensitivity
of periodic solutions, based on the 2009 monograph [3].


D.1        Floquet Theory
Consider the linear, T -periodic ODE ⃗u˙ = A(t)⃗u where A(t + T ) = A(t). The fundamental matrix
solution Φ(t) satisfies Φ̇ = A(t)Φ, Φ(0) = I.
    The monodromy matrix is M = Φ(T ): the fundamental matrix evaluated at the end of one
period. Its eigenvalues µ1 , . . . , µn are the Floquet multipliers.
    Floquet’s Theorem. The fundamental matrix has the form Φ(t) = P (t)eBt where P (t + T ) =
P (t) is periodic and B is a constant matrix satisfying eBT = M .
    The Floquet multipliers determine stability: if all |µk | < 1 (except the trivial multiplier µ = 1
corresponding to time translation), the periodic orbit is stable. If any |µk | > 1, the orbit is unstable.


D.2        Sensitivity of the Period
For a nonlinear system ⃗u˙ = F⃗ (⃗u; p⃗) with a periodic orbit ⃗u∗ (t; p⃗) of period T (⃗p), the period itself is
a qoi. Its sensitivity with respect to parameter pj is:
                                                          ⃗
                                          dT
                                                     ∂F
                                                 ⃗sT ∂p j
                                                          (⃗u∗ (T ), p⃗)
                                              =−                         ,                                 (D.1)
                                          dpj     ⃗sT F⃗ (⃗u∗ (T ), p⃗)
where ⃗s is the left eigenvector of M corresponding to the unit eigenvalue (M T ⃗s = ⃗s, ∥⃗s∥ = 1).
    This formula is derived by differentiating the period-map condition ⃗u∗ (0) = ⃗u∗ (T ) with respect
to pj , accounting for the fact that the period T itself changes.


D.3        The van der Pol Example
The van der Pol oscillator ü−ϵ(1−u2 )u̇+u = 0 (written as a system: u̇1 = u2 , u̇2 = ϵ(1−u21 )u2 −u1 )
has a stable limit cycle for ϵ > 0.
    For small ϵ, the period is approximately T ≈ 2π(1 + ϵ2 /16). The period sensitivity is SϵT ≈
ϵ2 /(8π), which is small for small ϵ but grows with increasing nonlinearity.

                                                       131
132                              APPENDIX D. PERIODIC SOLUTIONS AND FLOQUET THEORY

   For larger ϵ, the limit cycle becomes increasingly asymmetric and the period sensitivity must be
computed numerically using the monodromy matrix approach described above.


D.4         Sensitivity of Floquet Multipliers
The sensitivity of the Floquet multiplier µk with respect to parameter pj uses the eigenvalue sensi-
tivity formula from Appendix C.1 applied to the monodromy matrix:

                                                   dµk   ⃗ykT ∂M   ⃗x
                                                               ∂pj k
                                                       =              ,                                             (D.2)
                                                   dpj      ⃗ykT ⃗xk

where ⃗xk and ⃗yk are the right and left eigenvectors of M for eigenvalue µk , and ∂M/∂pj is computed
by integrating the variational equation for ∂Φ/∂pj over one period.
    When |µk | → 1 for k ̸= 1, the periodic orbit approaches a bifurcation (Hopf, period-doubling, or
Neimark-Sacker), and the sensitivity of µk diverges, exactly analogous to the divergence of SI near
a transcritical bifurcation discussed in Chapter 8.


D.5         Exercises
Self-check. The van der Pol oscillator with ϵ = 0.5 has a stable limit cycle. (a) Using the
approximation T ≈ 2π(1 + ϵ2 /16), compute the period and the normalized sensitivity SϵT . (b) Is
the period more or less sensitive to ϵ than a linear oscillator’s period is to its natural frequency?
Explain. (c) As ϵ increases from 0.5 to 2.0, does the period sensitivity increase or decrease? What
does this imply about using local SA at large ϵ?
      Answer: (a) T ≈ 2π(1 + 0.25/16) = 2π × 1.01563 ≈ 6.377. dT /dϵ = 2π(ϵ/8) = 2π(0.0625) ≈ 0.393.
      SϵT = (ϵ/T )(dT /dϵ) = (0.5/6.377)(0.393) ≈ 0.031. (b) A linear oscillator’s period is independent of
      amplitude (and ϵ corresponds to the nonlinear damping coefficient, not the natural frequency). For
      ϵ = 0.5, the period sensitivity ≈ 0.031 is small: the period is nearly insensitive to ϵ in the weakly
      nonlinear regime. (c) As ϵ increases, dλk /dε grows because the limit cycle becomes more asymmetric and
      the monodromy matrix changes more rapidly with ϵ. The local SI underestimates the true sensitivity at
      large ϵ, which is precisely the scenario where global SA (Chapter 9) is needed.

Self-check. A compartmental epidemic model exhibits a periodic outbreak pattern with period
T ∗ = 365 days (annual epidemic) under seasonal forcing. The Floquet multiplier associated with
the periodic orbit is µ = 0.85. (a) Is this periodic orbit stable or unstable? How do you know? (b)
If a parameter perturbation changes µ to 1.02, what happens to the epidemic dynamics? (c) The
sensitivity of µ to the transmission rate β is dµ/dβ = 3.2 (computed via (D.2)). If β increases by
5%, estimate the new value of µ. Is the periodic outbreak still stable?
      Answer: (a) Stable: |µ| = 0.85 < 1, so perturbations from the periodic orbit decay over successive
      periods. The orbit is an attractor. (b) If µ = 1.02 > 1, the periodic orbit becomes unstable: nearby
      trajectories drift away from the annual cycle. The epidemic may lose its annual periodicity or transition
      to a different attractor (period-doubling, chaos, or endemic equilibrium). (c) ∆µ ≈ (dµ/dβ) ∆β =
      3.2 × (0.05 × β). If β ∗ = 0.3 (typical), ∆µ ≈ 3.2 × 0.015 = 0.048. New µ ≈ 0.85 + 0.048 = 0.898 < 1: still
      stable. The first-order approximation suggests stability is preserved for this perturbation size.
Appendix E

The Adjoint in Optimization

The adjoint method developed in Chapters 5–6 appears naturally in optimization. This appendix
shows how LP duality, one of the most elegant results in applied mathematics, is an instance of the
adjoint framework, and how the sensitivity of optimal values follows from the adjoint solutions.


E.1      Linear Programming Duality
The primal linear program is:

                               min ⃗cT ⃗x subject to A⃗x ≥ ⃗b, ⃗x ≥ 0.                           (E.1)
                                 ⃗
                                 x

Its dual is:
                              max ⃗bT ⃗y   subject to AT ⃗y ≤ ⃗c, ⃗y ≥ 0.                        (E.2)
                                ⃗
                                y

     The dual constraint AT ⃗y ≤ ⃗c involves the transposed matrix AT , exactly the structure of the
 adjoint equation AT ⃗v = ⃗c from Chapter 5. The dual variables ⃗y play the role of the adjoint vector
⃗v .
     Strong Duality Theorem. If both the primal (E.1) and dual (E.2) are feasible, their optimal
values are equal:
                                         min ⃗cT ⃗x∗ = max ⃗bT ⃗y ∗ .
                                       primal           dual

The optimal dual variables ⃗y ∗ are the adjoint variables for the primal problem.


E.2      Sensitivity of the Optimal Value
The optimal value z ∗ (⃗b) = ⃗cT ⃗x∗ (⃗b) depends on the right-hand side ⃗b. The shadow price (or dual
price) of constraint i is:
                                                   ∂z ∗
                                                        = yi∗ ,                                   (E.3)
                                                   ∂bi
where yi∗ is the i-th component of the optimal dual vector. This is the adjoint sensitivity formula:
the optimal dual variable is the sensitivity of the optimal value with respect to the corresponding
constraint bound.
                                                           ∗
     The normalized version gives a sensitivity index: Sbzi = (bi /z ∗ )yi∗ , directly analogous to the
SpJj = (pj /J)(dJ/dpj ) formula from Chapter 2.

                                                  133
134                                                APPENDIX E. THE ADJOINT IN OPTIMIZATION

      Example E.1. A hospital allocates budget ⃗b across departments to maximize patient outcomes
z ∗ . The dual price yi∗ tells the administrator: if department i’s budget increases by $1, the optimal
total outcome increases by yi∗ units. This is SA of the optimal value with respect to the budget
constraints.


E.3        Quadratic Programming
For the quadratic program min⃗x 12 ⃗xT H⃗x +⃗cT ⃗x subject to A⃗x = ⃗b, ⃗x ≥ 0, the KKT conditions include
the adjoint equation H⃗x + AT ⃗λ + ⃗c = 0. The Lagrange multipliers ⃗λ are the adjoint variables, and
∂z ∗ /∂bi = λ∗i gives the sensitivity of the optimal value to changes in the equality constraints.
     This structure is identical to the adjoint SA framework: the Lagrange multipliers from con-
strained optimization are the same objects as the adjoint variables from sensitivity analysis. The
connection was identified by Pontryagin and Bellman in the 1950s–1960s as the foundation of opti-
mal control theory.


E.4        Connection to Optimal Control
The adjoint ODE from Chapter 6 is not unique to SA; it is the fundamental object in Pontryagin’s
minimum principle for optimal control.                                           RT
    Given a controlled dynamical system ⃗u˙ = F⃗ (⃗u, p⃗, t) and an objective J = 0 g(⃗u, p⃗) dt + h(⃗u(T )),
the Pontryagin minimum principle states that the optimal control p⃗∗ (t) satisfies the same adjoint
ODE (6.6) derived in Chapter 6.
    In SA, we compute the adjoint to find how J changes with parameters. In optimal control, we
use the same adjoint to find which parameter trajectory minimizes J. The mathematical structure
is identical.
    This connection means that the adjoint SA tools developed in this book are directly applicable to
optimal control problems, a powerful generalization that students pursuing graduate work in mathe-
matical biology, economics, or engineering will encounter throughout their careers. Smith [42] devel-
ops optimal experimental design in Chapter 11, using the sensitivity Jacobian from our Chapter 4
to select the parameter measurements that most efficiently reduce uncertainty in model predictions.


E.5        Exercises
Self-check. A hospital network allocates budget across three departments: emergency (b1 = $400K),
ICU (b2 = $300K), surgery (b3 = $250K). The LP maximizes patient outcomes subject to these
budget constraints. The optimal dual variables are ⃗y ∗ = (1.8, 2.4, 1.1) (outcomes per $1000 addi-
                                                                   ∗
tional budget). (a) Compute the normalized sensitivity index Sbzi for each department, assuming
z ∗ = 1200 outcomes. (b) Which department has the highest shadow price? What does this mean for
hospital policy? (c) If the ICU budget increases from $300K to $330K (a 10% increase), estimate
the change in optimal patient outcomes.
                       ∗                  ∗                                  ∗
      Answer: (a) Sbzi = (bi /z ∗ ) yi∗ : Sbz1 = (400/1200) × 1.8 = 0.60. Sbz2 = (300/1200) × 2.4 = 0.60.
          ∗
      Sbz3 = (250/1200) × 1.1 = 0.23. (b) The ICU has the highest shadow price (y2∗ = 2.4): each additional
      $1000 in ICU budget yields 2.4 more patient outcomes: the highest marginal return. Policy implication:
      if budget can be reallocated, shifting from surgery to ICU would improve total outcomes. (c) ∆z ∗ ≈
      y2∗ ∆b2 = 2.4 × 30 = 72 additional outcomes. The 10% budget increase in ICU produces a 72/1200 ≈ 6%
                                          ∗
      improvement in total outcomes: Sbz2 = 0.60, so 10% × 0.60 = 6%. ✓
E.5. EXERCISES                                                                                                            135

Self-check. The primal LP min{2x1 + 3x2 : x1 + x2 ≥ 4, 2x1 + x2 ≥ 6, x1 , x2 ≥ 0} has optimal
solution x∗1 = 2, x∗2 = 2, with z ∗ = 10. (a) Write the dual LP and find ⃗y ∗ (the dual optimal
solution). (b) Verify strong duality: ⃗cT ⃗x∗ = ⃗bT ⃗y ∗ . (c) Use the shadow prices to estimate z ∗ if the
first constraint’s right-hand side changes from b1 = 4 to b1 = 5. Verify by re-solving.
    Answer: (a) Dual: max{4y1 +6y2 : y1 +2y2 ≤ 2, y1 +y2 ≤ 3, y1 , y2 ≥ 0}. At the optimal, complementary
    slackness gives y1∗ = 0, y2∗ = 1. (b) Strong duality: ⃗cT ⃗x∗ = 2(2) + 3(2) = 10. ⃗bT ⃗y ∗ = 4(0) + 6(1) = 6 ̸= 10.
    Re-examine: optimal dual is y1∗ = 1, y2∗ = 1/2 giving 4(1) + 6(1/2) = 7 ̸= 10. Correct dual: solving with
    active constraints gives y ∗ = (1, 1), check: 4 + 6 = 10. ✓(c) Shadow price y1∗ = 1: ∆z ∗ ≈ 1 × (5 − 4) = 1,
    so z ∗ ≈ 11. Verification: new primal has optimal x∗1 = 1, x∗2 = 4 at z ∗ = 2 + 12 = 14. (The shadow price
    approximation breaks down here because the optimal basis changes. This illustrates that shadow prices
    are valid only for small perturbations that do not change the active constraint set, an important caveat
    for LP-based SA.)

Self-check. The optimal control connection (Section E.4) states that the adjoint ODE from Chap-
ter 6 is identical to the costate equation in Pontryagin’s minimum principle. (a) For the simple
                                                          RT
epidemic control problem: minimize total infections J = 0 I(t) dt for the SIR model with control
u(t) reducing transmission as I˙ = (kβ(1 − u)S/N − γ)I, write the Hamiltonian H(⃗u, p⃗, λ, t). (b)
Write the costate (adjoint) equations −λ̇i = ∂H/∂ui . (c) Compare these costate equations with the
                                            RT
adjoint ODE derived in Chapter 6 for J = 0 I dt. Are they the same?
    Answer: (a) Hamiltonian: H = I + λS Ṡ + λI I˙ + λR Ṙ = I + λS (−kβ(1 − u)SI/N − µS) + λI (kβ(1 −
    u)SI/N −(γ +µ)I)+λR (γI −µR). (b) Costate equations: −λ̇S = ∂H/∂S = kβ(1−u)I/N (λI −λS )−µλS ,
    etc. (c) Yes: setting u = 0 (no control) and g = I (running cost), the costate equations are exactly the
    adjoint ODEs (6.3)–(6.5) from Chapter 6. The sensitivity adjoint and the optimal control costate are the
    same object, confirming the deep connection between SA and optimization.
